B00AJER3SE|5.0|Having read some of the reviews here, this one might be a touch redundant.  I do not provide plot summaries and opinions about acting etc in any of my reviews but just write about the quality of the transfer to Blu Ray.  I had bought The Terminator on Blu Ray a day before the remastered version's release was announced and immediately returned the Blu Ray to the store and waited till the remastered version came out.<br /><br />The video transfer is certainly a big improvement over the original Standard Def release.  The video on this remastered version retains some of the original film grain from the film but nothing that is terribly noticeable unless you pause on an image.  There are no crawling ants in the grain or any artifacting to distract you.  The original film, which I saw in the theater when it was first released became a classic and deservedly so.  That said, some of the effects are very obvious, for example, when Arnold is looking in the mirror and working on his eye it is easy to tell that that is more animation than a real Arnold that we are looking at.  Technology has greatly improved since then so we just have to take obvious effects as the groundbreaking events they were at the time of their making.  Never the less,  the film looks great and, Yes, the color grading is considerably cooler than in previous releases.  Whether or not this is a good thing or not is not for me to judge.  After having seen the film so many times over the years, it was very noticeable to me but that doesn't make it better or worse.  That opinion is up to the individual viewer.<br /><br />The Audio is DTS MA 5.1 and it, as well as the subtitles, are supplied in several different languages.  The lossless and remixed audio, to me, is the star of this version as the editors went to town making sure that proper audio steering to a surround system was realistically done.  There is excellent use of both the front and rear sound stages with discreet channeling throughout the movie of various foley fx.  Your LFE sub channel will also get a decent workout as the bottom end is pretty deep at times.  The audio levels are properly set so that there is no need to roller coaster your remotes volume levels between action and dialogue shots.  Yes, there is no original mono mix for anyone who prefers that, but I do prefer this lossy surround mix myself. For me, this mix makes the audio DEMO worthy to show off your home theater's surround processor.<br /><br />The extras consisted of several 'Terminated Scenes' that may or may not have contributed to an extended version but there was nothing there that jumped out at me saying \\"put me back in\\".  There was also a pretty old Retrospective of the Terminator consisting of a discussion between Arnold and James Cameron.  I really didn't think too much of it but there were some interesting points made by both.  I did not know that Arnold was originally to be the hero of the film as Michael Beine eventually became.<br /><br />Personally, since I never saw the original blu ray release I cannot say whether, if you already have it on blu ray you should upgrade to the remastered version.  However, if you only have it on Standard Def, then I do think this is a very worthy upgrade.<br /><br />Hopefully, this review has been of some help to you in determining your purchase.<br />Thanksvarious foley fx. B00BMUYLSE|5.0|As with a lot of people reading this,I was not expecting David Bowie to make a follow up recording to his previous album [[ASIN:B0015XWUCC Reality]],which he released a decade ago. All the evidence in fact pointed to some type of semi retirement but that was not the case. He was apparently working secretly on this album,careful not to get too much of a pre publicity campaign going. How like Bowie to exercise such a meticulous level of control over the presentation and promotion of his art. He's a very difficult artist to follow,so honestly I don't know a whole lot about the production of this album or why it came to be when it did. All I know is that Tony Visconti is involved and that the cover art presents itself as a peculiar cut-n-paste variation on the [[ASIN:B00001OH7V Heroes]] artwork-with a simple square white box with the album title covering his face and the original title x'd out. So what has Bowie apparently (or allegedly depending on one's viewpoint) been up to in the creation of his next musical offering? And does it expand it any way from his 70's and 80's music?<br /><br />  Surprisingly it does. This is not Ziggy Stardust or the Thin White Duke. It's very much David Bowie,already in fact a character as his last name is Jones,obviously. He starts out with the title song,a very rhythmic dance/rocker that like the first single from this \\"The Stars (Are Out Tonight)\\",\\"Valentines Day\\" and \\"Dancing Out In Space\\",\\"Boss Of Me\\" and \\"(You Will) Set The World On Fire\\" define their strong song craft and melody by those layers of discordant drums,guitars and bass lines Bowie has always managed to combine in his music to create something somehow flowing and coherent. Of course being who Bowie is,there are many many surprises here as well. And those are actually among my favorites here. They include \\"Where Are We Now?\\",this expansive mixture of high pitched 80's style keyboard orchestrations with this rather stark Cabaret pop/rock style melody. There's also \\"If You Can See Me\\",which has a compelling mixture of Bowie's late 90's flirtation with drum 'n bass with the late 70's industrial dance/rock he pioneered on his Berlin trilogy.<br /><br />  \\"How Does The Grass Grow\\" is probably my favorite song here as it expands on that sonically heady fusion of pop/rock with electronica that defined his mid to late 70's musical explosions. \\"Dirty Boys\\" and \\"Love Is Lost\\" both have what I'd call an industrial blues flavor to them-the former funkier and the latter more stripped down. \\"I'd Rather Be High\\",\\"You Feel So Lonely You Could Die\\" and \\"Heat\\" are all intense and often very soulful epics that,in classic Bowie style,resist any real attempt at classification. Of the bonus tracks \\"So She\\" and \\"I'll Take You There\\" are both wonderfully orchestrated pop numbers whereas \\"Plan\\" is a two minute textured guitar instrumental. David Bowie and Tony Visconti are again at their best here creating this often very epic musical structures that are somehow intense and direct at the same time. They are the perfect setting for Bowie's typically rebellious romantic notions that define his lyrical send ups-either character plays or caricatured deconstructionism depending on ones viewpoint. While some might disagree,this is definitely an album worth waiting for. Not only does it have Bowie's ear catching sound musically,but there's a great deal of potential for an innovative type of commercial success as well. And in a more money driven music world,that's very much needed.Can See Me\\",which has a compelling mixture of Bowie's late 90's flirtation with drum 'n bass with the late 70's industrial dance/rock he pioneered on his Berlin trilogy.<br /><br />  \\"How Does The Grass Grow\\" is probably my favorite song here as it expands on that sonically heady fusion of pop/rock with electronica that defined his mid to late 70's musical explosions. \\"Dirty Boys\\" and \\"Love Is Lost\\" both have what I'd call an industrial blues flavor to them-the former funkier and the latter more stripped down. \\"I'd Rather Be High\\",\\"You Feel So Lonely You Could Die\\" and \\"Heat\\" are all intense and often very soulful epics that,in classic Bowie style,resist any real attempt at classification. Of the bonus tracks \\"So She\\" and \\"I'll Take You There\\" are both wonderfully orchestrated pop numbers whereas \\"Plan\\" is a two minute textured guitar instrumental. They are the perfect setting for Bowie's typically rebellious romantic notions that define his lyrical send ups-either character plays or caricatured deconstructionism depending on ones viewpoint. While some might disagree,this is definitely an album worth waiting for. 0385528752|5.0|Yesterday, I sat in my office thinking about my boss' latest attempt at implementing an organizational change that, given the tenor of the department, has a small likelihood of success.  That got me thinking about my most recent set of New Year's resolutions, which (as of mid-March) are lying in ruins.  These failures left me wondering:  why are some changes so hard to make?  Having finished Switch, I now know why many changes are hard to enact, while a few changes are relatively easy.  Chip and Dan Heath depict the psychology churning in the background of a change attempt, describe the factors that sabotage a desired change, and present an effective plan for countering these factors.  The Heaths make their conclusions memorable by utilizing both a \\"down-to-earth\\" writing style and an impeccable narrative structure.  Both of these tools reinforce the lessons that the Heaths discuss, making the strategies they present accessible to the reader (and thus, more likely to succeed when utilized).  Most effective in driving home their points are the various examples of successful change they present.  The examples not only lend an optimistic tone to the text, but also inform the reader that sweeping change is possible once one understands obstacles to change and how to overcome them.  Even with these examples and tools, I recognize that successful change is still not guaranteed. Still, the comprehensive approach and detailed guide that the Heaths present in Switch give me reason to believe that the next attempt at change in which I'm involved (either personal or organizational) will be more successful than previous attempts.

The book does not have anything that can be seen as too violent or sexual for middle school students. I reread The Giver for a college course, and I gained a greater understanding and love for the book doing so. One of the themes is perfect for young adults: finding one's individuality. Jonas is different from the rest of the community is many ways, so a young adult will gravitate towards Jonas because many young adults feel they are unlike the people around them.<br /><br />  Medication is mention moderately as an unnatural thing, which can reflect the growing number of young adults who are being put on medication (for ADD/ADHD), or to make the young adults rethink pill-popping to get high. Another, more sophisticated theme is about free will.  All of the citizens are give rolls and rules which they must follow. They must abide by these rules without fail, even if that includes euthanizing the old or unruly babies. The Giver says, \\"They know nothing,\\" meaning the citizens have no power to moderate themselves because they are products of what others tell them to think and do. Totalitarianism is what the community is portraying.<br /><br />  1984, another book I thoroughly enjoyed, is similar to The Giver because in both books there is no sex or love allowed; the setting is futuristic; the main character is a rebel; and the citizens are always monitored through speakers , cameras, or people keeping tabs on them. If you enjoyed 1984 chances are you will enjoy The Giver as well.<br /><br />  What I noticed while reading The Giver is that I appreciated many of the freedoms that I have but the citizens of Jonas' community do not have, especially how I can see in color while the citizens of Jonas' community cannot. Ending are always importantfor me, and The Giver really delivers. There are a few ways the end can be interpreted which lets the reader decide the desired outcome, or if you don't like to decide, let it be a small mystery.

B000UG4LYU|4.0|I walk into the music section and there it is. Like a flying saucer from out of nowhere, it sits on the shelf in front of me. People have talked about, they have seen photos of it, but hardly anyone has ever heard it. It is not supposed to be there, not supposed to exist, but there it is anyway.<br /><br />That was 2007. The album that was released in 1993 was at long last available for public consumption. It has been my most played Bowie disc since that time. The last *new* Bowie album I bought until \\"The Next Day\\" came out yesterday. It is decidedly un-commercial. Which is part of the reason I like it.<br /><br />\\"Black Tie White Noise\\" reached #1 in the UK but failed to sell well in the US, partly because Bowie's American record label was having so many problems. Because of this Bowie did not tour for it and was able to turn right around and work on another project. The book \\"The Buddha of Suburbia\\" was being turned into a miniseries for BBC TV and Bowie was asked to supply the soundtrack. Bowie completed several short works made from combining guitar, synthesizer, trumpet, percussion and sitar.<br /><br />A month later he teamed up with Erdal Kizilcay, extending those short works into longer loops and adding overdubs and vocals. In a week he had composed a new album. Released in the UK and Europe in November 1993 as a soundtrack album, not a Bowie album, his best album in a decade was never heard. Yet this is David Bowie's favorite David Bowie album, and the one that got him back into making more creative albums. It is a transitional album, tying together \\"Black Tie\\" and \\"Outside\\".<br /><br />Slow to fast ~ the album running order:<br />1 The Mysteries<br />2 Ian Fish UK Heir<br />3 South Horizon<br />4 Sex And The Church<br />5 Untitled No. 1<br />6 Strangers When We Meet<br />7 Buddha of Suburbia<br /><br />`The Mysteries'. Like the sun rising down a hallway in an Egyptian temple, this song evokes wonder. It is at once the most beautiful, elegant and understated tune that he has ever recorded. This was accomplished by a wash of synth matched with backwards piano and guitar passages. Then the recording was slowed down to produce the desired, dreamy effect.<br /><br /> `Ian Fish UK Heir'. I don't know who he is but when you first hear his song it just seems like noise. It is deceiving, because it is actually layered with instruments. The first would be the plucked acoustic guitar. If this was all you heard you could say it was a traditional song. But far in the background you can hear a piano running a set of high notes over and over. Then there is the static. Is this to make it sound like a vinyl record, or like machinery coming over your radio? The machinery is supplied by what sounds like three synth parts. There is the sound of something spinning. There is a long drawn out siren like sound. And there is the actual drone which makes up the bedrock of this song. All in all it is a very interesting piece for those who take the time to decipher it.<br /><br />The end of this drone meshes well with the start of `South Horizon', as we hear a jazz band warming up. Piano, bass, cymbals and drums get into a groove. Two and a half minutes later the actual song starts. This is Bowie's favorite song on the album. While the two ambient instrumentals, `Mysteries' and `Ian', sound like backing tracks on \\"Outside\\", `South' would feel right in place on \\"Black Tie\\". Like `Looking For Lester', this song is a cool groove, a happy little shuffle.<br /><br />It ends with tickling piano, and now we hear a keyboard warble. A percussive beat and treated vocals leads us into `Sex And The Church'. We have suddenly gone from ambient to jazz to progressive music as easily as walking from one room to another. Once more we have a happy bouncing groove as Bowie wonders about the joys of the flesh coexisting with religion. This song would fit right next to `Pallas Athena' on \\"Black Tie\\". It ends with a woman trying to catch her breath after the act. And telling you she loves you.<br /><br />Now we come to `Untitled No. 1'. This song is much more poppy but the overall sound fits in well with what we have just heard. What started off as a mellow album has now become loud and up front with this bopping number. All is happy and well as Bowie's treated vocals tell us next to nothing as to what this song is about, what is he saying? \\"City of fools... it's clear that some things never take\\"? And this seems to be what he is getting across to us.<br /><br />The bounce becomes more pronounced now as `Strangers When We Meet' hits home. Speaking of home, Bowie mentions his flat screen TV, and this in 1993.  What is weird that the `Strangers' we hear here is much more power progressive here than the piano version found on \\"Outside\\", which according to each albums flavor, is backwards from what we should expect. Bowie re-recorded it for that album because this album, due to record company problems, was never going to be released. He knew he had a winner and he wanted it heard. Why he wanted to lighten it up I do not know, but it fits much better at the end of \\"Outside\\" because it provides a much needed relief from the bludgeoning one goes thru after an hour of listening to that album.<br /><br />At last we get to the Buddha himself. `Buddha of Suburbia` may be the best rock song here, with tight acoustic guitar, bass and synth grounding us to an early period Bowie. His early instrument the saxophone, gets the first solo, while his later instrument, the guitar, gets the last one. It is a good way to end the album on a high note. It is all about being English and growing up in the suburbs, then moving on to the city of London, which seems to be what the book was all about and what Bowie actually did. He had seen UFO's when he was young, and the song ends with a flying saucer taking off. Do yourself a favor, take the trip.<br /><br />Now if he would just release a proper recording of all the missing \\"Outside\\" takes.<br /><br />4.5 Starsnd telling you she loves you.<br /><br />Now we come to `Untitled No. 1'. B00AYHKIZ6|4.0|The Next Day<br />You open the album already influenced by \\"Heroes\\", and that is exactly what you start with. If I heard this a few months ago I would have sworn it was an outtake from that album, or \\"Scary Monsters\\" at least. Think rough rockers like `Beauty And The Beast' and `Joe The Lion'. He may be older but you would never know it. It ends too quickly though.<br /><br />Dirty Boys<br /> A sexy strut for a cigar girl, or a cartoon cat, making their way thru a smokey club, past a combo of sax, bass, drums and a snarky guitar. Like \\"Monsters\\", the second song sounds a bit different, but like `Sons' sounding different then `Beauty', it still fits. Probably due to the production, as the sound seems to match. Like the first track, it is too short.<br /><br />The Stars<br />Starting out with a 1:52 intro that is very \\"Heathen\\" sounding, the video version of this seems more of a complete song. For some reason they left the intro off the album version. As it is, this still sounds very heathenish to me, with a dose of \\"Reality\\". Whether that is due to the production or the vocal style or just the overall sound, I do not know. If I had heard this song not knowing there was a new album I would have thought it was an outtake from either of those albums. Many of the trademarks of those last two albums can be heard. Where \\"Heathen\\" was more basic, leaning more on mood, \\"Reality\\" had more going on, which at times was too thick. This has the requisite rock instruments as well as what sounds like a synthesizer laying a tone over all the satyrs and their child wives. A good rock song, one which already sounds familiar.<br /><br />Love Is Lost<br />Moody and full of beat. Weird backing vocals. Would have fit next to `She Drives The Big Car', might even be about the same girl. Definitely a studio track, probably not something that would ever be played in concert. Nice guitar touches that seem like they are coming down the hallway.<br /><br />Where Are We Now<br /> This one is too smutchy for me. Maybe because it is the only one that shows his age. The drumming is nice, but it does not do much but yearn, which may be the point of it. It seems out of place. Won't be on my playlist.<br /><br />Valentine's Day<br />This is guitar rock from another era for sure. The song, like his vocals, sounds like it was recorded in the seventies. Way too short. I guess we should be happy that the older crowd will be listening to this; otherwise we could expect another senseless shoot up on Valentine's Day. Should school killings be getting turned into songs? Won't that just make them want to do it more?<br /><br />If You Can See Me<br />Here is one not suitable for younger listeners. This is the wack track. Skittish drumming and hurry up vocals. I might not be playing this one either.<br /><br />I'd Rather Be High<br />Another early seventies song. How does he do this with his voice? He has not sung this high for ages. Solders lying in heroine fields, who would rather get high than kill. If you can picture the Spiders doing a Vietnam song, then you will have this one down.<br /><br />Boss of Me<br />Low key Sax again, for a boy stuck on a girl. The slinky pace makes this one. It would fit better right after `Love Is Lost', as it has that slower, measured feel to it. It is not country, but this is a song that a country artist would want to record for their own. Look for it on \\"Nashville\\" next season.<br /><br />Dancing Out in Space<br />I must have cowboys on the mind, because I can see this as a line dance. Another out of nowhere track. Are we doing the fifties now? Starts off good but dumb lyrics drag it down. `Lucy Can't Dance' is much better. I am surprised he did this one. Skipper.<br /><br />How Does the Grass Grow?<br />Another wack track moment, as the \\"Lodger\\" shows up out of nowhere. This one is hard to fathom in its changes. Some good beats with party down chants. Might have been instantaneous fun to play, but it is going to take a while to nail this one down. Those voices grab you though.<br /><br />Set the World On Fire<br />And we are back to \\"Scary Monsters\\" again. This should be a fan favorite. Chorus oriented guitar rock that most people could sing along to the first time they hear it. If he does a concert you can expect to hear this. This and 'Stars' may be the only songs that can stand up with `Everybody Says Hi\\" and \\"New Killer Star\\".<br /><br />You Feel So Lonely You Could Die<br />This is a good song but this too seems out of place. The `Five Years' ending does not help. Would be stronger going from `Fire' to `Heat'. Skipper number four.<br /><br />Heat<br />David Bowie doing Johnny Cash? This is a cross between `Bring Me The Disco King' and Duran Duran's \\"Dirty Great Monster\\". Which is a good thing, but it is not as strong as either of those. A toned down ending to an album that is much better than it should be, if that makes any sense. Looking at al the 5 star reviews I thought I might at least like this one. I am really impressed. This is really good!<br /><br />So She<br />Sorry Bon Jovi, but Paul Anka has dibs on this one. Bowie takes us back to the fifties in polka dot style. Too short to make much of a dent, more of an after thought<br /><br />The Plan<br />This short droning instrumental should have been used as an intro to the album. That is where I will play it. Oh sneaky David! I just realized that this is the intro to the `Stars' video. Alright! Would have made a good album opener to ease you into things.<br /><br />I'll Take You There<br />Apparently he's not afraid of Americans anymore. Talk about radio friendly, this sounds like a travel ad. This is the catchiest song here. This one should have been on the album. It is way better than `Where' and `See', though it is way too short. I spent about twenty minutes in the store trying to decide if I wanted to spend the extra three bucks on the Deluxe Edition. I cannot make that judgment for you, but for me, these last two make it worth it. I will be playing these last two songs in place of those I skipped.<br /><br />So is Bowie's new album groundbreaking? No. But it is a welcome extension to his catalogue. Better than I expected it to be. His last two albums had two songs that I skipped, so in that regard it is not up to the same level, but this one has more songs. \\"Next Day\\" seems to be all over the place at times and is in dire need of re-sequencing to make it flow better. Individually, the songs may be stronger than \\"Heathen\\", whose power lies in the moody images evoked by the entire album as a finished piece. Can they match the best songs on \\"Reality'? Maybe.<br /><br />The images evoked here are of \\"Heathen\\"s, Heroes\\", \\"Lodger\\"s and \\"Scary Monsters\\", with a touch of \\"Reality\\". If you liked those albums, this new one should appeal to you.  As strong a comeback album as most could have wished for. At the very least it wins the prestigious \\"Worst Album Cover of the Century\\" award.s one down. B0085A9HXS|5.0|THE RAID: REDEMPTION is one of the most successful movies I've seen in YEARS.  It's not the greatest movie, by a long-shot...but the gap between its ambition (which is pretty high) and its actual accomplishment is almost non-existent.  I've long believed that movies need to be judge, in part, on how well they accomplish what they attempt, and not just how \\"good\\" they are by some high-brow, artsy standard.  Thus, a low-brow comedy like KNOCKED UP can get 5 stars from me because while it isn't great art, it meets or exceeds all its own goals.  And by the same token, a gorgeous, ambitious, \\"serious\\" film like, oh, let's say ANNA KARININA only gets 3 or 4 stars, because its reach so far exceeds its grasp.<br /><br />THE RAID: REDEMPTION is darn near perfect.  It wants to be a nearly non-stop, adrenalin-fueled, ultra-violent, martial-arts heavy action movie.  It tries to strike a balance between incredible tension and the visceral release of that pent-up tension.  It wants to shock and awe.  And it does.<br /><br />Set in Indonesia, we meet a group of heavily armed and armored cops who are planning a raid on a very tall apartment building.  At the top of this building sits the city's most vicious crime lord, along with his two cruelly effective henchmen.  Beneath them, for floor after floor, is a seemingly endless string of bad guys, the scum and villains of the town.  The cops must fight their way from bottom to top...and once their presence is exposed the viciousness and intensity of the fight is nearly ceaseless.<br /><br />The movie, while not based on a videogame, is the best example of a videogame movie I've seen.  Granted, that's a low bar...but THE RAID clears that bar so effortlessly.  The action sequences, which start with gunplay and then devolve first to knife play and then hand-to-hand combat, are among the best I've seen.  I've never been a huge fan of the MMA/martial-arts type of combat, where the hero and his foe kick, punch, block, flip, etc.  But I've NEVER seen so many fights staged so brilliantly or executed with such brutal fervor.  This is no exaggeration...during some of the action sequences, I found myself on the edge of my seat, talking to the screen (or yelling at it).  I simply DO NOT do that, yet I couldn't help it.  I was engrossed and engaged.<br /><br />Yes, there is a bit of a story.  We are introduced, perfunctorily, to several characters and we do learn a few things about them.  There is even a bit of a surprise relationship that is revealed late in the film.  There are moments for dialogue and for catching a breath.  I wouldn't say the dialogue is brilliant or the acting Oscar caliber...but it is a little better than one might expect.  Iko Uwais plays Rama, our young hero...and he's a convincing screen presence (and a helluva fighter).  This is the kind of movie that relies on archetypes to gives us a shorthand for understanding the characters.  The heroic rookie, the guy with the smart-aleck remarks, the leader with a secret, the potentially corrupt higher leader, the insane bad guy, etc. etc.  What THE RAID does, however, is take these stock characters and put them in nearly perfect motion for maximum impact.  Gareth Evans wrote, directed, edited and co-choreographed the film.  In my mind, his is one of the most singular accomplishments in recent film history.  Again, his goals are not exactly lofty or ennobling.  But I can barely conceive how he managed to succeed so well where so many others have tried and failed.<br /><br />This is a brutal film, and many viewers will not be able to finish watching it.  Some will find it glorifies violence or is just deeply depressing in its depiction of insane violence.  For me, it was a rush from start to finish.  It was a punch to the gut, but it was somehow uplifting as well.  It's simplicity of plot and tightness of purpose set it up to succeed beyond anything I could have expected.  I had heard it was good, but was skeptical.  \\"Surely that's just fanboys and genre nuts talking,\\" I said to myself.  But I was wrong.  Evans and his talented team delivered one of the most thrilling movies I've seen.  Wow!

0553593714|5.0|Let me start of by saying that I have not finished this book but I couldn't wait to start my review.  As I continue the book, I will add to my review.  This is not my typical review as I tend to write about the book as a whole but this time I want to concentrate on the central characters in this first book.<br /><br />As you read the reviews already posted, most will tell you what the book is about so I don't even need to go there.  I will however add that this book had me hooked from the very first page.  I can rarely say that of many books.  I wholeheartedly agree with all who say this is one of the most engrossing story ever.  I believe the reason for this book sucking you right in is due to the portrayal of the characters.  GRRM is a genius storyteller that gives all of his characters depth so that you feel strongly about each of the main characters.<br /><br />Here are is my summary (I suspect most will agree with me but feel free to disagree):<br /><br />Ned Starks-The King's hand and Lord of Winterfell.  Perfect man for the job although I squirmed along with him when he was offered the position.  How can you say no to the King who is also your best friend?  He comes across as cold, serious and rather moody at time but I like him.  You can tell that he is a man who will do what is right, no matter what the cost.  That is integrity.<br /><br />Catelyn Tully-Ned's wife.  I can't really relate to her as a woman.  She doesn't come across as warm, caring person.  You only really see her maternal side when she is dealing with her son Bran.  What really made me not like her as a woman was the way that she deals with Jon Snow.  Jon is Ned's illegitimate son.  As a woman, I can understand her emotions towards Jon but as a mother I know that you can't make a child pay for the father's sins.  Her obvious hatred of Jon made me dislike her.  I think I might have liked her a little if she had hidden her intense dislike for Jon because I do understand her anger and jealousy but she just flaunts her hatred in his face making me feel bad for Jon.<br /><br />Robert Baratheon-Although he is king, he certainly doesn't act it.  I think he likes the title but not the responsibilities that go with being King of the Seven Kingdoms.  He isn't a bad guy but he doesn't have a backbone so he really disappointed me.  Maybe now that Ned is by his side, he will step up to the plate?  I don't think so but one can hope.<br /><br />Queen Ceresei-Cold-hearted bitch (need I say more???).  I hope one of the dire wolves kill her just like she had one killed.<br /><br />Jamie Lannister-Ceresei's twin brother.  I think he might have turned out not as evil as his twin if only he had cut the umbilical cord (metaphorically speaking) that binds him to his sister in more ways than one.<br /><br />Tyrion (the Imp) Lannister- Ceresei and Jaime's brother.  He is continually ostracized by everyone and hated by his own sister (see what a cold-hearted bitch she is) because he is a \\"midget\\" and an ugly one at that.  I like him very much as I think he is smarter than the twins and unlike them, has a heart.  He also provides comic relief in a book that is full of drama, betrayal and suspense.  What's good about his character is that although he is a Lannister, I am not sure where his loyalty will lie once they are truly tested.  If I am right about him, I believe he will do what is right and not do what is wrong out of family loyalty.<br /><br />Jon Snow-Ned's bastard son.  One of my favorite characters because he knows his place in his father's world and he doesn't go around feeling sorry for himself because he is a bastard.  He acts more like a true born than some of his half siblings.  I see good things in his future, if only he survives.<br /><br />Bran-One of Ned's younger son and Catelyn's favorite child.  I like him because he doesn't seem to have a bad bone in his body but I don't know what's going to happen with him.  Don't want to give out any spoilers.<br /><br />Ayra-Ned's youngest daughter, a skinny, scrawny scrap of girl that will end up surprising us all.  I like her because she is a rebel.  I think that she will be like her father and do the right thing.  I like that she treats everyone equally including Jon and seems to have more royal blood in her veins than her older sister, Sansa who is to marry the King's son, Joffrey.<br /><br />Sansa-Ned's oldest daughter-prissy princess.  She is her mother's daughter as she treats Jon like a bastard rather than a half brother.  That alone makes me not like her.  She also doesn't stand up for what is right and blames her sister for everything that goes wrong.  The fact that she is engaged to Prince Joffrey just sealed my dislike.<br /><br />Prince Joffrey-King Baratheon's oldest son and heir.  He is as priggish as Sansa is prissy.  They were made for each other.  If they inherit the kingdom, the kingdom is doomed.<br /><br />Danaerys-Dragon princess.  Forced to marry a total stranger in order to help her brother regain his throne.  I feel sorry for her but I think as the story progresses, she will summon up the courage and make her brother stop taking advantage of her and take her rightful place as the remaining Dragon Princess.<br /><br />Viserys-Dragon prince.  I understand his anger but I could not like him for selling his sister in order to regain his throne.  It is the custom of Dragonstone that the prince brother should marry the princess sister in order to keep the royal blood line pure but in this case I am glad that he doesn't because he is a bully and too wrapped up in his misery and angry to make a great king much less husband.  I hope Danaerys inherits the kingdom because a man so obsessed with revenge will only destroy himself rather than those he seeks to avenge the death of his family.<br /><br />There are many more characters in this book but so far these are the main ones who have grabbed my attention, one way or another.  As I continue the book, I might change my mind about how I feel about the characters as GRRM has a way of surprising us. You might end up loving a character you first hated and that's what so good about this book, you really don't know where it's going.  Don't you love reading a book that is totally unpredictable?  Of course we all love happy endings but in real life, the unexpected happens and life isn't always a fairy tale.  Maybe that is why I am enjoying this book so much, my first fantasy book.<br /><br />PS.  I bought the first season DVD and let me tell you....almost as awesome as the book.  Of course I am reading the book first and watching an episode or two to see if it stays true to the book and it does.  The special effects and cinematography is out of this world (PUN INTENDED).<br /><br />As always, feel free to comment on my review.  I like hearing people's opinion even if they don't agree with me.>Ayra-Ned's youngest daughter, a skinny, scrawny scrap of girl that will end up surprising us all. B005LAIIGC|5.0|Arrived on time and as expected; package had been abused a bit but the contents were in perfect shape.  Good job on the packaging, Amazon!    The good parts of this review: The eggs are too cute. The movie comes with Blu-Ray, Standard DVD, and a Digital copy unlocked by using Ultraviolet.  While i don't use Ultraviolet because their system was still buggy when I first bought something that used it (Green Lantern) and I still have problems getting through their system, I don't need the digital copy at this time.  Because it's not necessary to my enjoyment, I only detract a half-star and cannot 'round down' here, so.  This is generally a good packaging of a very good movie.  I'm not going to review the movie itself... well, OK, a little.    The cultural characters of Santa Claus, the Sandman, the Tooth Fairy, the Easter Bunny, all came from a series of beautifully illustrated, fanciful children's books, by William Joyce, which are described here at this website: [...]    Joyce recreates the characters completely from their embed in real human historical things - there is no Saint Nicholas of Myra in this Santa, there is no rabbit of the Goddess of Spring in the Easter Bunny, there is no analysis of why Christmas or Easter deserve prominence, because it's not about the religious background.    For some people this will be bad. For others this will be good. Until I knew that this was from a series of stories, I didn't know that it wasn't just a deliberate removal of the religious background from two of these characters, but the focus of the movie was on the extremely engaging character of Jack Frost, voiced by Chris Pine.  So far as I know Jack Frost is not part of the books (yet).    Jack Frost is intangible and inaudible except in the way he exerts his power of cold, because nobody believes in him. He's a \\"bad boy\\" - a very mischievous youth (at 317) who was changed by the Man in the Moon - another character from the original books, who appears in the movie only as the full moon, and never speaks aloud so that we can hear him.    The movie tells three stories.  The first is a story of Jack Frost - the young man whose delight in the fun of living is being eroded by the constant reminder that he is not 'real' to the people around him.  He knows he was made by the Man in the Moon, but doesn't know anything about himself from the moment he awoke underwater in a partially-frozen pond, dragged out of the fear and the dark by the brightness of the moon. His coming into himself is the first story; the second is his becoming, and being accepted, as a Guardian of Childhood, one of the elite mythical beings who inspire the positive feelings of childhood.  The third story is that of 'the boogy man' - Pitch, the Nightmare King, ruler of darkness of fear and doubt and pain who wants to spread them over the world, who has slowly grown stronger since his last defeat. He has returned, and he has everything he needs to overcome the Four Guardians... except for this interloper Jack Frost, who isn't sure he wants the job of Fifth Guardian.    Even if you don't like the secularization - the story is filled with positive, uplifting narrative, with strong moral values.    The packaging is cute, though a bit disposable, and the eggs are amusing.  Now for the less thrilling aspects, the production of the Blu-Ray disk and the content wrapper.  You may skip this if you don't enjoy rants, because I do rant a bit there at the end.    There were two \\"interactive games\\" and a number of language subtitle options, including director's information, which I will be exploring later.  For now, though?  The movie was good, and all I needed was to make popcorn to make it as enjoyable as the movie theatre experience.  Playback was not as simple as it should have been.    Fortunately, I watch blu-ray movies using a blu-ray drive on my personal computer.    As happens with many Blu-Ray products, I once again had to upgrade my commercial Blu-Ray software because in the never-ending paranoid attempt to explain to us how we're all thieves, the movie industry has made it yet again a format incompatible with my previous software.  At this point, I suspect that any Blu-Ray player I would have purchased before last year would be worthless to play this movie.  Further, they hammer the viewer with several mandatory warnings, threats, and bluster-fests wherein they explain that the world is ending because people don't respect copyright.  It must be fun living in a world where everyone caters to your whims and terribly upsetting when they stop putting up with you (and I'm talking to the Studio people here) but in the real world, people don't respect people who start out by showing their own disrespect in the first place.    Make stuff available that people want and they will pay well to see it. Make crap and people will probably pay what it's worth.  Make good stuff unavailable, and people will find a way to get it.  That's been true for as long as recorded civilization.    The FBI warning? Unnecessary, reading it doesn't make me do anything but resent you.  The bit at the end with the earnest message of  \\"how you can go educate yourself (because you're obviously an ignorant pirate) about how PIRACY has been destroying our ECONOMY\\" ... uhm, yah, no.  What's been destroying our economy is a combination of things involving greed and overreaching, but one big part of it has been fraudulent acts on the part of banks that grossly inflated then collapsed a major part of our economy to below the point before the inflation was started.  That's an entirely different kind of piracy.    But I promise that I won't take the DVD and Blu-Ray disks that I just PURCHASED for MONEY and make copies of them for everyone else, if you promise to start looking at realistic ways to make piracy not worth it, by making it easier to get your stuff and by making it easy for people to pay you what they feel it's worth - and that's usually more than you'd get otherwise if it's any good.    One Hint: Continually changing formats, messing up things with DRM, and generally insulting your customers?  That won't do it.

0634022296|5.0|I've got a lot of Beatles songbooks, and this is by far my favorite. And the chord diagrams show the chords as they're actually played on the record, as opposed to doing silly things like calling a standard D chord raised a half step an Eb (technically correct, but no one ever plays an Eb like that). The real test for me with Beatles books is Norwegian Wood: do they show the tonic as E played like a regular E, or do they correctly show it as a D capo'd to the second fret? If its not the later, the chord diagrams will be useless in the book. This one gets it right.<br /><br />Another good contender is The Beatles - Complete Scores, but the writing is so small, and since it shows so much info on each page, most songs span at least 10 pages. So I use that book for cross referencing and seeing solos or melody lines if I need it, and this for general song outlines.

B00AYHKIZ6|4.0|David Bowie makes one of the most anticipated returns of 2013 releasing his first album in just shy of ten years, [[ASIN:B00AYHKIZ6 The Next Day (Deluxe Edition)]], released via Columbia. The Next Day proceeds his previous studio album, September 2003 release [[ASIN:B0000C8AUO Reality]]. Over the course of 14 tracks, mostly written solely by Bowie, the 66-year old rock veteran rarely, if ever, missteps. The Next Day ends up being an enjoyable reminder of Bowie's pivotal role within the music world.<br /><br />Title track \\"The Next Day\\" starts things off strongly with a driving groove, characterized by jagged guitar and pounding drums.  Bowie's vocals are energetic, though later cuts possess more fine tuned vocal production.  The catchy chorus brings things together: \\"Here I am / not quite dying / my body left to rot in a hollow tree / its branches throwing shadows / on the gallows for me / and the next day / and the next / and another day.\\"  \\"Dirty Boys\\" opts for a more relaxed, even more hip groove. The sound is steeped in psychedelic/soulful/ art rock, noted for its gritty, dirty electric guitars and the thoughtful use of saxophone.  \\"Dirty Boys\\" manages space well; there is plenty of space for instruments to play between lyrics/lines.  Bowie vocally delivers the sophomore cut soundly.  The uptempo \\"The Stars (Are Out Tonight)\\" caps off the first trio of cuts superbly, opting for a driving groove favoring the opener.  The combination of acoustic and electric guitars works well creating a rhythmic wall of sound.  The lyrics continue to allure, with Bowie's chorus highlighting.<br /><br />\\"Love Is Lost\\" continues the utmost consistency, notable for magnificent production and dramatic songwriting.  \\"It's the darkest hour, you're twenty two / the voice of youth, the hour of dread / the darkest hour and your voice is new / love is lost, love is lost,\\" Bowie sings on the verse.  The groove drives, sporting minimalism at its best.  Promo single \\"Where Are We Now\\" is simply beautiful, contrasting everything else as of yet with rock balladry.  The harmonic progression features thoughtful chordal choices while Bowie delivers arguably his most tender vocal of The Next Day.  The chorus is simplistic, but telling: \\"Where are we now, where are we now? The moment you  know, you know, you know.\\"  Pummeling drums add more rhythmic intensity towards the end, punctuating lyrics \\"As long as there's sun...\\"<br /><br />\\"Valentine's Day\\" proceeds, taking a more 'standard' approach compared to other cuts.  Even so, it still possesses the Bowie touch.  \\"If You Can See Me\\" is incredibly animated - his most manic, unique number to an extent.  Instrumental riffs continue to propel, as do unique lyrics: \\"I have seen these bairns wave their fists at God / Swear to destroy the beasts, stamping the ground...\\" \\"I'd Rather Be High\\" is optimistic and anchored down by a gargantuan bass line, a direct product of the 70s.  The chorus is predictably strong (\\"I'd rather be high / I'd rather be flying / I'd rather be dead / or out of my head...\\"), aided by the use of harmonized backing vocals.<br /><br />\\"Boss of Me\\", co-written with Gerry Leonard, has a dark air about it as Bowie sings \\"...Who'd have ever dreamed / that a small town girl like you / would be the boss of me...\\" \\"Dancing Out in Space\\" rolls right along, while \\"How Does the Grass Grow?\\" delivers one of the knockout punches of The Next Day.  \\"How Does the Grass Grow?\\" possesses many classic rock cues, arguably delivering the best production work of the effort. The chorus is both addicting and tongue-in-cheek: \\"Ya ya ya ya ya ya ya ya ya ya...how does the grass grow / blood, blood, blood...\\"  Clever, clever, clever.<br /><br />\\"You Will Set The World On Fire\\" has a hard act to follow, but continues consistently without conceding momentum.  The 6/8 groove of the penultimate \\"You Feel So Lonely You Could Die\\" is a welcome contrast, while the use of strings is a lovely touch.  A unifying refrain doesn't appear till the end (\\"I hope you could feel so lonely / you could die...\\").  Concluding number \\"Heat\\" closes with a mysterious vibe, different from anything else appearing before it.  The key lyric \\"My father ran the prison\\" reappears throughout the course of the cut. (The Deluxe edition features three bonus tracks \\"So She\\", \\"Plan\\", and \\"I'll Take You There\\".)<br /><br />Overall, [[ASIN:B00AYHKIZ6 The Next Day (Deluxe Edition)]] is another fine addition to the David Bowie discography.  Not only does Bowie make a return, it is a triumphant one filled with solid songs and solid vocals.  This album probably won't stand the test of time of his classics (most modern albums by veteran artists don't given the loftiness of previous work), but it is nothing short of enjoyable or consistent.  4 stars.pear till the end (\\"I hope you could feel so lonely / you could die...\\").  Concluding number \\"Heat\\" closes with a mysterious vibe, different from anything else appearing before it.  The key lyric \\"My father ran the prison\\" reappears throughout the course of the cut. (The Deluxe edition features three bonus tracks \\"So She\\", \\"Plan\\", and \\"I'll Take You There\\".)<br /><br />Overall, [[ASIN:B00AYHKIZ6 The Next Day (Deluxe Edition)]] is another fine addition to the David Bowie discography.  Not only does Bowie make a return, it is a triumphant one filled with solid songs and solid vocals.  This album probably won't stand the test of time of his classics (most modern albums by veteran artists don't given the loftiness of previous work), but it is nothing short of enjoyable or consistent.  4 stars.

B004EPZ07U|4.0|3.5 out of 5<br /><br />When dying alien and Green Lantern Abin Sur is discovered by brash and cocky fighter pilot Hal Jordan (Ryan Reynolds), Hal's life is suddenly changed when the mysterious alien gives him a green power ring and matching lantern with vague instructions to \\"speak the oath.\\"<br /><br />After finally unlocking the lantern, Hal is taken to the planet Oa where he learns he has become Abin Sur's successor in the Green Lantern Corps and is also the first human to ever bear the powerful mantle of a Green Lantern.<br /><br />As part of his training, Hal is taken under the wing of a powerful Lantern named Sinestro (Mark Strong) whose view of right and wrong is sheer black and white, and who has no trouble enforcing the law with lethal force. Turns out Sinestro wasn't the first to feel this way as long ago one of the creators of the lantern rings--the Guardians of Oa--disagreed with the Oan Council and set off on his own, discovering a new power, this one the yellow power of fear. Now the super powered being Parallax, this former Guardian wishes to take revenge on those who banished him.<br /><br />As Hal learns what it means to set aside his own pride and ego and live by the sacred Green Lantern oath--In brightest day, in blackest night, no evil shall escape my sight. Let those who worship evil's might, beware my power, Green Lantern's Light!--he must come to grips with his newfound power and expel Parallax's presence from the universe once and for all.<br /><br />After the crazy success of Batman Begins and The Dark Knight, Warner Brothers and DC Comics were in big need of another hit after Superman Returns failed to deliver at the box office, and so they went to another DC hero: Green Lantern. Good choice. He's a kind of Superman/Batman hybrid in that Hal Jordan is human and has the qualities and struggles thereof like Bruce Wayne, and yet by wielding his power ring, his superpowers get up there right alongside the Last Son of Krypton in many ways. Whether this was Warners' reasoning or not, I don't know--probably not--but GL was certainly a good character to try and take to the big screen especially since it had never been done before.<br /><br />In a nutshell, the movie wasn't bad. I liked it. It didn't change my life, but it's not the piece of garbage many folks make it out to be. It covered Hal Jordan's transformation into Green Lantern, delivered awesome effects, created a sense of atmosphere both about the Green Lantern Corp and Oa, and came through on telling a simple story that got Hal Jordan from Point A to B in a reasonable amount of time.<br /><br />People complained there wasn't enough action or not enough stuff on Oa--but those kinds of things are--and weren't--supposed to be the focus of this movie. It was about getting the ring into Hal's hands and teaching him the ol' Uncle Ben motto of \\"With great power comes great responsibility.\\"<br /><br />I like how it took time to get Hal used to using the ring and it wasn't a case of him putting it on and suddenly becoming an expert on creating green light constructs. And once he figured it out, I enjoyed how his constructs were simple--the racetrack, machine guns, etc.--as opposed to something crazy or way too technical. Why? Put yourself in his shoes. You'd probably construct something you're more comfortable with than trying to create some big complicated airship stocked with robot soldiers with a zillion weapons and stuff.<br /><br />The love story between Hal and Carol Ferris (Blake Lively) seemed forced though it did provide a nice bridge between the realm of Oa and Earth. Clearly this relationship was introduced for sequel purposes because those who know the comics know Carol Ferris becomes the supervillain Star Sapphire down the line.<br /><br />I think in the end, Green Lantern did its job. Could it have been better? Sure. Could it have been worse? Yup.<br /><br />Regardless, I like popping this movie into the player from time to time, and if you'rea superhero fan, you should, too.a superhero fan, you should, too.

Then hundreds of pages about band formation, first hits, first girlfriends, drugs, stardom, drugs, getting married, drugs, sex, drugs, auto-destruction, nearly death experience, clean-up, drugs, clean-up, drugs, clean-up and divorce. For closure, new girlfriend and reconstruction.<br /><br />My first impression was to consider Pete's book a conventional autobiography that chronologically tells the story of his life. About halfway through the book, I realized that Pete's writing is way more deeper than my instincts prematurely judged. Pete didn't only talked about his life, he dragged me into his life. All of the sudden, against my wishes, I escorted him, side by side, in his adventures across the world.<br /><br />\\"Who I am\\" is not light. Very often is depressive and dark. Unlike Keith Richards, Pete didn't invest much time talking about his sweet side. Pete is also less subtle. Often because he tries to be honest with himself, he comes through as aggressive, saying things that hurt him, his friends and us.<br /><br />That was not unexpected. I know his songs well enough to have built an image not so far from what Pete portrayed himself. That is the guy from \\"I am the one\\". Self-pity, complexed, depressed,  traumatic, lonely. Judging from this book, Pete is uptight.<br /><br />What distinguishes Pete from Jagger/Richards, Lennon/McCartney or even Dylan is that Pete was the only one that was able that consistently write about about himself, his fans and his generation in one song. From \\"I Can't Explain\\" and \\"My Generation\\", through Tommy, Lifehouse, Quadrophenia, Empty Glass, Chinese Eyes and maybe today (I stopped to follow him after White City). Keith Moon death, for example. He put aside only 2 or 3 pages for that (very depressing pages by the way), but he built the momentum for that climax. We knew it was coming and eventually it came. I found absolutely brilliant  the way he described how he was informed of Keith death.<br /><br />This  is a story of a very compelling man that couldn't explain his love, was so frustrated by life that wanted to die before getting old, moved out from home, but had the washing done by his mom, looked for a big spiritual force, made millions, constantly looked for love, lost millions, looked for death, looked for life again, always giving blood. In the end, we don't want to be in the shoes of our old idols.

B00AYHKIZ6|5.0|The Short Story:<br />If you are new to David Bowie's work, this isn't a bad album to start with, but I might begin with some of his work from the 1970's.  If you are an established Bowie fan, this album is a must-listen.  It's some of Bowie's best material in decades.<br /><br />The Longer Story:<br />At the end of Paul Trynka's Bowie biography \\"Starman,\\" he leaves with a speculative note.  David Bowie never said he retired, but after his A Reality Tour, he disappeared from the music scene almost completely.  This was unusual for the singer/songwriter - he would rarely go a few years without releasing an album in his decades-long career. It seemed like Bowie had genuinely retired, and Trynka's biography leaves some hope that one day, Ziggy Stardust himself will awake from his slumber and release an album that would blow everyone away.<br /><br />Then in early 2013, the world received word: David Bowie was not only working on another album after almost a decade of silence, but the album was already finished and coming out in a couple of months.  The album announcement was accompanied with a video for \\"Where Are We Now?\\"  The song is a slow, introspective ballad that creeps along, and it presents a problem: this is not a song for young, up-and-coming Bowie fans, and it doesn't give listeners a good idea of what the album is.  This is a song that reflects on Bowie's life, and this is significant in the eyes of a fan because as a songwriter, Bowie rarely lets his guard down.  Even personal songs like \\"Changes\\" are wrapped in heavy melodies and pop production.  \\"Where Are We Now?\\" hints at an album that finds Bowie in his later years, reflecting back on his career.  At this point, I was expecting THE NEXT DAY to be an album full of songs like \\"Thursday's Child\\" - good, mellow, wise, but missing the spark that his earlier material had.<br /><br />Fortunately, \\"Where Are We Now?\\" gives absolutely no indication as to what THE NEXT DAY sounds like.  With its opening track (the title track), the album roars to life with energetic guitar riff not unlike something from Bowie's Berlin triptych.  Most of the music here is mid to up tempo, and a lot of it reminds of stuff that would have been recorded around 1975-1980.  \\"If You Can See Me,\\" \\"Dancing Out in Space,\\" and \\"Dirty Boys\\" sound like they could have come from the same period as well. \\"How Does the Grass Grow?\\" has a choppy rhythm that feels pulled out of the 1980's oeuvre.  There are a handful of songs that sound strange, but only in the sense that they sound unfamiliar.  \\"The Stars (Are Out Tonight)\\" and \\"Valentine's Day\\" don't have the quirk or glam that has defined most of Bowie's songwriting, but they are great traditional rock songs with strong melodies.  All of this is played with a renewed sense of interest: Bowie didn't make this record because he was fulfilling a contract, and he's not going through the motions.  He doesn't sound bored here - he sounds more excited than he has in a long, long time.<br /><br />The album finds Bowie pushing himself forward into new territory but with an eye on the past.  Many of these songs do sound new and fresh, but I wouldn't be surprised if some of these were actually written in Bowie's days in Berlin.  The album art encapsulates this: it's the cover of HEROES, but with a giant white square obscuring his face.  Even though Bowie might have (in his work in the 2000's) tried to avoid reaching into his past, he's realized he can't.  Instead of running from his past, and instead of embracing it, he does something different.  He accepts the past and tries to one-up himself.  There's an acceptance from Bowie that we've seen from him in more of the recent albums, but nothing this clear eyed.<br /><br />The album feels like it's the most personal Bowie has ever released.  \\"The Next Day\\" uses a chorus that begins with: \\"Here I am / Not quite dying / My body left to rot in a hollow tree.\\"  The song is a triumphant (and knowing) return to form.  Bowie isn't rejecting his age here, he's embracing it and using it as a personal challenge.  \\"Where Are We Now?\\" is a meditation on a former life, back in Berlin in the late 1970's.  \\"Heat\\" has a few haunting moments in its slow, paranoid crawl as well.<br /><br />For what it's worth, THE NEXT DAY is the best (and most consistent) album that Bowie has released since 1980's SCARY MONSTERS.  This album feels like Bowie is comfortable with his own legacy; we see plenty of the trademark Bowie hallmarks here.  I don't feel like there's any standout single like \\"Changes\\" or \\"Starman\\" or \\"Heroes,\\" but this album makes up for it in its consistency.  I would recommend this album to any fans of Bowie's, but I think that newcomers will find a lot here to enjoy as well.  The fact that this album exists at all is a wonder, but the fact that it's a great one is more than I can ask for as a fan.<br /><br />Essential tracks to sample/listen: \\"The Next Day,\\" \\"Valentine's Day,\\" and \\"The Stars (Are Out Tonight).\\"<br /><br />Additional release information:<br />THE NEXT DAY was also released in a deluxe edition.  The deluxe edition of the album comes with three songs that do not appear on the full version.  These songs are \\"So She,\\" \\"Plan,\\" and \\"I'll Take You There.\\"  Out of these three songs, one of them is an instrumental: \\"Plan\\" appears on the \\"The Stars (Are Out Tonight)\\" video as the opening minute and a half before the video begins proper.  The remaining two tracks are good, but they don't quite compare to the other songs on THE NEXT DAY.  I'd recommend this version for the Bowie fanatic, but it isn't essential listening.ie isn't rejecting his age here, he's embracing it and using it as a personal challenge.  \\"Where Are We Now?\\" is a meditation on a former life, back in Berlin in the late 1970's.  \\"Heat\\" has a few haunting moments in its slow, paranoid crawl as well.<br /><br />For what it's worth, THE NEXT DAY is the best (and most consistent) album that Bowie has released since 1980's SCARY MONSTERS. There is a reason Jeff Lynne is one of the most admired and trusted personalities in the music industry, despite some of his critics. Jeff is close friends with all the Beatles and their partners alive and passed, Bob Dylan, Tom Petty, Eric Clapton, Elton John, Brian Wilson, Duanne Eddy, Joe Walsh, and Randy Newman, among many others.<br /><br />Jeff Lynne and The Electric Light Orchestra are a classic fixture in many adults lives, and in spite of some scathing reviews in the past, ELO provided not only a soundtrack to our lives, but influenced hundreds of current musicians around the globe.<br /><br />This is a magnificent collection of 11 planets you can visit, in any order you like depending on your tastes. B005LAIII0|5.0|The Short: A visual marvel. A human triumph. A test of faith. Finding God. An incredible story. Life of Pi is this and many other things, sweeping the viewer away into a mesmerizing place that blurs the lines between dream and what is real.<br /><br />Ang Lee directs the previously thought un-filmable book to the big screen, Life of Pi. How it comes to life is breathtaking; an ingeniously woven tapestry of incredible visual elements and powerfully adept storytelling, all to the affect of discovering the greater meaning of being alive in a situation you would rather die in.<br /><br />It's an art movie that many audiences will love simply because of the gorgeous visuals, memorable character(s), and incredible spectacle. But for those looking for a deeper, meaty, and memorable movie going experience Life of Pi has you covered. No other movie nominated for best picture at the 2013 Oscars has perplexed me, moved me, made me reflect and think, and swept me away within its lush, visual imagery and unique storytelling. It's a film chock full of metaphor, visual imagery, and raw emotion. But this isn't a discussion of the film's many layers of religious undertones and imagery, but a review. I'll keep it that way!<br /><br />First off is director Ang Lee's incredible talent on display (for which he won Best Director - 2013). I have yet to read Life of Pi but from what I've heard from friends and book junkies is that Life of Pi is an incredibly complex, almost muddy in direction, novel which many considered un-filmable. What becomes of this epic tale of survival is a clean and expertly directed film, with all of its themes and underlying tones hidden within the visual landscape and story. It's not a force-fed experience, but rather one where you must process the vast array of information and make your own judgment on some of its less touched-on ideas. Whatever creative process Mr. Lee went through to bring this incredibly complex adventure to life is beyond me, and that's a true testament of the genius on display.<br /><br />You can't talk about Life of Pi without discussing the incredible visuals. Winning both Best Cinematography (2013) and Best Special Effects (2013) at the 2013 Academy Awards, I had high expectations going into Life of Pi. The film starts off, simply put, like any other PG-rated family film. I was nervous that it was going to be a bust. Where the film takes the viewer is someplace completely unique and unexpected. Without spoiling some of the finer moments, Life of Pi has visual panache to spare. There are some incredible moments that are rendered and recreated with such life-like detail that getting lost in Pi's tale is tremendously easy. Its visuals dazzle, but don't distract its storytelling, unlike other films (namely Avatar, which is a visual marvel but hardly a groundbreaking example of storytelling). One of the main strengths in Life of Pi is Richard Parker, the Bengal tiger that accompanies Pi during his quest. Little did I know that that 90 percent of his screen-time is completely computer animated. And it's not just that, but the rest of the film; the water effects, the tumbling ship, various animals; all of it is incredible. It maintains a perfect balance of realism and a dream-like quality. Some of the scenes are also shot with incredible vision and beauty. It's simply the most beautiful cinematography of any film last year (other than the Hobbit). I have nothing but praise for Life of Pi's cinematographer and special effects team.<br /><br />But no story is worth being told without great characters and actors to tell them. Life of Pi's Suraj Sharma (Pi) is absolutely fantastic. Emotional, powerful, and sincere; he is one seriously talented actor for never having been in a role before...ever. While the beginning of the film features a quick romance between Pi and Gita, featuring its fair share of awkward exchanges, its nothing annoying. Pi and his incredible story is what you see 90 percent of the time. It's his story of finding faith in the darkest of times, discovering courage where none should be present, and having the will to continue to live. The rest of the supporting cast does just fine in their various roles, aiding along the telling of a most grandiose adventure.<br /><br />Everything else is spectacular as well. The original score (also winning the Oscar for Best Score - 2013) fits the pages of Pi's story like clockwork. From the moving \\"Pi's Lullaby\\" to the gorgeous and ethereal \\"First Night, First Day\\", the original score never distracts but rather augments the experience, creating some of the most beautiful audio-visual-scapes in any film from recent memory. Sound editing is particularly fantastic. Crashing waves, minute sound effects; it's all done particularly well, especially in its transition to home video (aside from a couple of weird boom adjustments made mid scene that distract the acute ear). And boy, I can only imagine the splendor of the 3D this film had to offer but I, regretfully, was not able to see it in theaters, only on Blu-Ray. The one issue I had with the visual presentation was this, also noticed by Blu-Ray.com: \\"There are two spots in the film where the aspect ratio changes suddenly from the normal 1.85:1. During the \\"flying fish\\" sequence that was used as a teaser for the movie, the top and bottom close in to 2.35:1, and there's a single overhead shot of a whale passing beneath the lifeboat that inexplicably squeezes in to a window-boxed 1.33:1. I'm not sure what the rationale or workflow was here, so I can't really comment on it, but don't be alarmed by the out-of-nowhere framing changes.\\" In layman's terms, the film goes from a whole-screen experience to having the black bars on the top and bottom and sides during two scenes, which is fairly distracting.<br /><br />Where Pi finishes his journey is up to you to discover and interpret. Where it stands for me, personally, is my favorite film of 2012. Visually spectacular, incredibly engaging, intricately told, and masterfully directed, Life of Pi was a massive gamble that pays off in more ways than one. It's an absolute classic.<br /><br />5/5 - Spectacular, Sensational; insert your own positive descriptor here and go see this movie!t's his story of finding faith in the darkest of times, discovering courage where none should be present, and having the will to continue to live. B000ICM0YY|3.0|(3.5/5 stars)<br /><br />Emil Gilels' recordings of Beethoven's sonatas were left incomplete due to his untimely passing in 1985. They are, however, complete enough that this collection withstands comparison to truly complete sets. As such, one must first acknowledge what is missing: #1 in F minor (Op. 2/1), #9 in E major (Op. 14/1), #22 in F major (Op. 54), #24 in F sharp major (Op. 78), and #32 in C minor (Op.111). The most glaring omission is the final sonata, but I would not be without the others either. It's hard to believe if you haven't experienced them, but there is no filler amongst Beethoven's published sonatas. The early electoral sonatas thrown in by DG have some curiosity value but do little to compensate for the missing published sonatas.<br /><br />There is a lot to be said for Gilels' powerful, virtuosic and flexible approach to the music, which never strains credibility. Sometimes the results are spectacular (examples: big, dramatic `Pathetique' and `Appassionata' sonatas, a virtuosic `Waldstein', and a rollicking Op. 10/3), but while he works within a successful template his playing often lacks warmth and sensitivity. The result is a  clinical `Hammerklavier', a bumbling first movement to the `Pastoral', a rather bland pair of Op. 2 sonatas, and the list goes on.  There are exceptions to these characterizations: His hushed pensive opening to Op. 109 defies the \\"vivace\\" indication, yet it is undeniably moving and his recording of that sonata remains a favorite. His Op. 110 is similarly convincing, conveying sensitivity and subtlety of feeling. Overall though, as I have conducted comparisons with other complete sets (Arrau, Goode, Gulda, and Kempff - mono), I have gradually lost interest in Gilels' recordings. Technically and intellectually he is a master of this music, but his playing lacks heart and spontaneity.<br /><br />The recordings were spread over the course of more than a decade and include the transition from analogue stereo to digital, yet the sound is strikingly uniform. Although very clear and well balanced, it has a clangy quality  that casts an edgy glare over the music. It's no showstopper, but it tends to exacerbate Gilels' lack of tonal warmth.<br /><br />There are many choices in this music, and although Gilels' was my first set of Beethoven sonatas it is not one I would recommend. If you are looking for virtuosic edge-of-your-seat playing, Gulda (on Eloquence) or Goode will satisfy while also conveying the warmth and spontaneity that are lacking in Gilels' set. If it is depth and introspection you seek, I recommend either Arrau (Decca) or Kempff (mono, DG import), each of which finds the heart of this music in their own way.

0385349947|5.0|From the public discourse surrounding this book, I was expecting a polemic, elitist tone. It is anything but. It is a great read with a good combination of personal stories, social research, business advice, and social commentary. While Sandberg has been criticized for focusing on the psychological, rather than institutional, barriers that prevent women from attaining leadership positions, she is very clear in the first few pages that she full recognizes the very real institutional barriers that exist - it's just not what this book is about. She is focused on the ways women hold themselves back, on pervading gender stereotypes and judgements that impact and are perpetuated by both men and women, and on how to change these. She is very clear that she believes work to change both the institutional and psychological barriers needs to occur together.<br /><br />As for the argument that this book is elitist, that seems very unfair. Yes, Sandberg is very wealthy and can afford lots of help. Does that mean she shouldn't share her experience? She is one of the few women at her level of executive leadership, and part of the power of this book is the personal stories she tells. She doesn't make any claim that her experiences are relevant to everyone, and in fact, continually references the fact that she knows they are not. She is focused on women in business, and possibly government, that have the potential and desire to move up the ladder (or jungle gym, as she makes a case for changing the metaphor.) And for that audience, and anyone working in these arenas, this book has a lot of interesting things to say.<br /><br />So who is the audience for this book? I would say anyone working in a corporate environment, any woman looking for career advice, and anyone interested in gender issues. I fall into the third category, although at one time I fell into the first two, and am now one of the women who made a different choice from Sandberg - I left the corporate workforce when my children were born. I resonated with so many of her corporate stories, but as a one-time stay-at-home and now work-at-home mom, I also didn't feel the least bit put-off by anything she said. She avoids the 'mommy wars', and makes a great case for ending them altogether.<br /><br />You may not agree with everything Sandberg says, as I did not, but her book will make you think, and want to discuss, what she says. Isn't that what good books do?hildren were born. Isn't that what good books do?

0062014536|5.0|If you've been reading the Delirium series from the start, you've probably been dying for this final book to come out.  I'm such a fan of this series, and of Lauren Oliver as a whole, so I've been recommending the series to pretty much anyone who would listen.  And I couldn't get it open fast enough the day it came out.  Fortunately, I had just finished a book so I was completely ready for the wonderful world that Lena and Hana live in.<br /><br />Prior to reading this novel, I knew the perspective was going to alternate from Lena to Hana, and I wasn't sure how I felt about that.  I didn't love the way Pandemonium flowed, which alternated from Lena's present to her past, so I was worried I would have similar issues with this one.  However, for whatever reason, it worked really well for me as I was reading.  Lauren Oliver does a great job of weaving the stories so they contribute to the novel as a whole, each growing closer together in time until they come together.<br /><br />As the reader, we've essentially been following Lena's story from the start, and at the beginning of the novel, hers picks up pretty closely to where it left off in Pandemonium.  Since leaving Zombieland and coming to the Wilds, Lena has been slowly becoming a part of the revolution, whether she wants to be or not.  To a large extent, she seems to just want happiness, but no one can really survive in the Wilds on their own, so the few people who aren't interested in the fight sort of get brought into it anyway.  She's also dealing with an internal struggle of her own - how does she deal with the return of Alex when she's got Julien, who is new to the Wilds and in some ways, only came along with them because of his feelings for Lena.  In a sense, Lena is to Julien what Alex was to her.  She would have gone along with the cure had it not been for meeting Alex, and we as the reader don't really know what Julien ultimately would have done had it not been for Lena.<br /><br />Hana's story is somewhat new to us, although if you read the short story Hana, you'll see some of the same information.  We pick her thread up after she has had her procedure, in the weeks leading up to her marriage to Fred Hargrove.  I really enjoyed the differences in how Hana thought about things versus Lena - Hana mentions feeling guilt for some of the things she did that previous summer, and expresses fear that perhaps her cure didn't quite work, but everything with her is a bit muted.  I felt like we really knew Lena this whole time, because although she's grown, she didn't get cured and she remains herself at her core.  Hana, on the other hand, is a bit unpredictable because she knows she's supposed to act in specific ways as a result of her cure, but there is that leftover something else underneath, so you never really know which side is going to win.<br /><br />My emotions were running pretty high throughout most of the novel, and I don't think I've cried that much during a book since reading the last in the Harry Potter series.  The story as a whole is just fantastic, and I loved pretty much every minute of it.  As is the case with all good series, I'm left wanting more, and I'm hoping that Lauren Oliver will write another series based on these characters.  If nothing else, we end not quite knowing what happened to several people, and I would love to find that out some day.  And there's the ever present question - regardless of the ultimate outcome, how do you rebuild your society after any of these events?t new to us, although if you read the short story Hana, you'll see some of the same information. B000LPQ6DE|4.0|I, too, an one of the uber-geeks who has seen nearly every film production of Jane Eyre, most of them severely wanting.  All leave out a plot point or two considered significant by us self-appointed guardians of the novel.  However, whether a film adaptation sports canyon-sized plot holes or remains sheepdog-faithful to the end, CASTING IS EVERYTHING.  The play of characters as they dance and collide,nuances of expression and gesture and all that is unspoken and not left to written dialogue--this is what matters.  Chemistry matters. And yes, the actors should bear some likeness to the characters they portray (example:  I respect George C. Scott, but as 35-year-old Rochester? Please.  A little hair dye does not erase the wrinkles and sags of age).  What's more, actors should understand their characters at an essential level, not just zero in on easily-identified traits (such as Rochester's seething, surly, uncomfortably intense side) and let those dominate the portrayal.  People are complicated.  Acting should reflect that. That's what ruined the Samantha Morton/Ciaran Hinds pairing.<br /><br />  To reiterate some of that review:  Want a better, more lush, fleshed-out, warm-hearted version? Although he does not answer the Rochester character in looks as well as Hinds, Tobey Stephens in the Masterpiece Theatre adaptation is way, way better: rude and gruff by turns,then vulnerable; guilty over his inability to be kind to Adele, with a tortured sense of duty to Bertha; his expressions and manner soften, his banter with Jane is amusing and the pivotal romantic scene is tender, fulfilling in the way it should be. Ruth Wilson herself is lovely, plain one moment and a shining pearl the next--perfect casting there. Forgive my directness,but you can picture Wilson and Stephens making love, and the thought does not make you shudder. There you have it. This should be a love story. The unexpected physicality--Jane Eyre usually gives us dry heat, not humidity--was a pleasant surprise.<br /><br /> Also well-wrought, although she is older than Adele is meant to be, was the young actress's portrayal of Rochester's poor little orphaned ward, with her opera-dancer mother's glittery affectations and flourishes, but, beneath the froufrou, a love-starved child seeking a parent.  The little \\"Georgie\\" girl who played Jane as a child, and who depicted Lucy so nicely in The Lion, the Witch and the Wardrobe, made the most of a smallish role:  her Jane was neither saccharine nor stiff, but the precocious, stifled-but-passionate ten-year-old who grew into the light-under-a-bushel young woman of the story.  Andrew Buchan was more appealing as St.John than I'd ever thought possible, making Jane's struggle well, more of a struggle.  And Bertha Mason is finally portrayed with some hint that she is not the grotesque she-devil that was the early-Victorian understanding of mental illness, but a tortured, once-beautiful woman condemned to imprisonment.  Her death scene is haunting rather than a \\"good riddance\\" moment, as it's been done in other versions.  Good supporting cast, and, although the post-Thornfield sequence with the Rivers family is a bit rushed, what there was was warm and charming.  Although I already knew the story,the Rochester-Jane reunion was nevertheless moving, Rochester's raw anguish-to-joy transformation evidence of the heart's ability to heal. Altogether, a satisfying production.
